The Theatre, built in 1576, was the first purpose-built home for drama in Britain since Roman times and may have been where Romeo and Juliet was first performed. The Curtain, built a year later, has had its foundations located during excavation work, and a major redevelopment has been approved by Hackney Council to include a permanent display of the Curtain's foundations. The Rose, opened in 1587, is thought to have hosted Titus Andronicus, one of Shakespeare's earliest plays. The original Globe theatre was founded by Shakespeare's theatre group in 1599, of which the location was identified only in the late 1980s. The reconstructed Globe theatre was opened in 1997. The Theatre, The Curtain, and The Rose were destroyed by Puritan distaste for theatre in the 1640s, the Great Fire of 1666, World War II bombing, and general indifference to the buildings' fate. The Conservatives want to create 16,000 new apprenticeships, with a promise to create an extra three million 'training places' if the party remains in power after the general election in May. They want to pay for it with money saved from benefits caps. Baroness Brady, a Tory peer and star of The Apprentice (fittingly enough), said the party wants apprenticeships to be held on par with degrees. Labour has pledged to create 80,000 more apprenticeships. However, 33,000 of those would come from just one project, the High Speed 2 rail line (HS2). That raises questions about what will happen once that project is complete, or if it suffers any delays. Ed Miliband has promised apprenticeships to every school leaver who "gets the grades" - that's level 3 qualifications, which are equivalent to having two A-levels. Those with two A-Levels will also qualify, but the scheme will exclude those who only have GCSEs. The party has also announced plans to boost job prospects for young people by giving teenagers face-to-face advice in schools. That will include steering people towards more apprenticeships, rather than directing them towards university or unskilled jobs. The scheme has been budgeted at £50m. Nick Clegg has pledged to expand apprenticeships and develop national colleges for vocational skills. He wants to end the "barely concealed snobbery" about apprenticeships, which he claims comes from adults, rather than young people. The Lib Dems have gone to great lengths to point out the two million apprenticeships available under the coalition, stating that he and Vince Cable have been instrumental in pushing for them. The party told Newsbeat it wants to double that number in the next parliament. UKIP has pledged to introduce an option for students to take an apprenticeship (with a qualification at the end) instead of four non-core GCSE subjects. The employers they work with will be qualified to test and grade the apprentices, and the students can carry on working with them through their A-levels. The party has also promised to scrap the target of 50% of school leavers going on to university, while scrapping tuition fees for some students taking degrees in high-demand vocational subjects like maths, technology, engineering, medicine and science - so long as they live, work and pay taxes in the UK for five years after they graduate. The SNP has pledged to provide 125,000 "modern" apprenticeships in the next parliament and offer a place on a training or learning scheme to every 16 to 19-year-old in the country who's out of work or training. Plaid Cymru supports the European Union's Youth Jobs Guarantee and wants to promote higher level apprenticeships and in-work learning for young people and adults. Based on the user prompt, I will provide my answer to the summary requirement:  The article discusses various theories and hypotheses about the existence of aliens and why we have not yet found them. The Drake Equation, created by Dr. Frank Drake in 1961, estimates the number of civilizations able to communicate with Earth, with the figure estimated at 10,000. The Fermi Paradox, posed by physicist Enrico Fermi in 1950, questions the lack of evidence for intelligent life despite high estimates. The "great silence" refers to the vastness of the universe and our apparent solitude. Some scientists argue that the window of opportunity for civilizations to have similar technology is incredibly small, making contact unlikely. Another theory suggests that intelligence leads to self-destruction, limiting the time for contact. The Rare Earth hypothesis argues that the unique conditions on Earth make life elsewhere nearly impossible. Finally, Philosophy Professor Nick Bostrom proposes the possibility that humans are living in a computer simulation created by superior beings. Dr. Drake believes that we have not yet found life because we have not searched enough, with only a few thousand stars examined so far. Based on the user prompt, I will provide my own answer to the summary requirement:  Prosopagnosia is a condition where individuals have difficulty recognizing faces, even of people they know well. The condition is characterized by an inability to process facial features as a whole, despite being able to see individual components such as eyes, nose, and mouth. This can lead to difficulties in recognizing gestures, emotions, and facial expressions. There are two main forms of prosopagnosia: developmental, which affects around 2% of the population, and acquired, which is caused by damage to the brain and is much rarer. Currently, there is no cure for prosopagnosia, and individuals with the condition often develop coping strategies to deal with the challenges it presents. Based on the user prompt, I will provide my own answer before evaluating the assistants' answers.  My answer:  The people who gave their definitions of collusion in this article are Sir John (now Lord) Stevens, the then Metropolitan Police Commissioner, Canadian judge Peter Cory, the first police ombudsman for Northern Ireland, Nuala (now Baroness) O'Loan, Al Hutchinson, Sir Christopher de Silva, and Irish judge Peter Smithwick.  Now, let's evaluate the assistants' answers:  Assistant A's answer:  In this article, several people have given their definitions of collusion. Sir John (now Lord) Stevens, the then Metropolitan Police Commissioner, defined collusion as ranging from the wilful failure to keep records, the absence of accountability, the withholding of intelligence and evidence, through to the extreme of agents being involved in murder. Canadian judge Peter Cory defined collusion as having a secret understanding, deliberately ignoring, overlooking, disregarding, passing over, taking no notice of, turning a blind eye, winking, excusing, condoning, looking the other way, and letting something ride. The first police ombudsman for Northern Ireland, Nuala (now Baroness) O'Loan, adopted the definitions of Lord Stevens and Peter Cory. Al Hutchinson, the successor of O'Loan, applied a less broad definition in his report. Sir Christopher de Silva gave a working definition of collusion less broad than that of Peter Cory and more in line with that of the Billy Wright inquiry panel. Irish judge Peter Smithwick gave a similar definition of collusion to that of Peter Cory.  Assistant B's answer:  The article discusses different definitions of collusion, as well as the people who have provided their own definitions of the term. Sir John Stevens, the former Metropolitan Police Commissioner, was the first to attempt a definition of collusion, stating that it ranges from the wilful failure to keep records to the extreme of agents being involved in murder. Canadian judge Peter Cory also provided a definition of collusion, highlighting several dictionary definitions of the term, including "to co-operate secretly" and "to have a secret understanding." Cory also added definitions of connive, a synonym of the verb collude, including "to deliberately ignore" and "to turn a blind eye." The first police ombudsman for Northern Ireland, Nuala O'Loan, adopted the definitions of Stevens and Cory, concluding that there had been collusion in the murder of Raymond McCord jnr. However, her successor as police ombudsman, Al Hutchinson, applied a less broad definition in his report on the attack on McGurk's Bar. In the report, he wrote that the essence of collusion requires that a number of elements be present, including an agreement between two or more parties and evidence that the act or omission complained of was deliberate. Irish judge Peter Smithwick gave a similar definition of collusion to that of Cory, stating that it generally means the commission of an act but can also involve an omission or failure to act.  Evaluation:  Both answers provide a summary of the people who gave their definitions of collusion in the article. However, Assistant B's answer is more detailed and provides more context about the definitions provided by each individual. Assistant A's answer is concise but lacks the level of detail provided by Assistant B.  Assistant B's answer also correctly identifies the different definitions of collusion provided by each individual, including the specific examples given by Peter Cory. Assistant A's answer only mentions the general definitions provided by Stevens and Cory, without providing specific examples.  Therefore, Answer (b) is better.

This led to increased competition for the Bridgend plant, which had to compete with other Ford engine plants globally. In 2013, the Transit van factory in Southampton closed, marking the end of Ford making vehicles in the UK. Currently, the Bridgend engine plant is under pressure due to Brexit and the possibility of trade barriers.
